                        cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Insights on the Mechanism of Na-Ion Storage in Soft Carbon Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jian, Zelang; Bommier, Clement; Luo, Langli; Li, Zhifei; Wang, Wentao; Wang, Chongmin; Greaney, P. Alex; Ji, Xiulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">29</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2314-2320</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0897-4756</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite is the com. anode for lithium-ion batteries; however, it fails to extend its success to sodium-ion batteries.  Recently, we demonstrated that a low-cost amorphous carbon-soft carbon exhibits remarkable rate performance and stable cycling life of Na-ion storage.  However, its Na-ion storage mechanism has remained elusive, which has plagued further development of such carbon anodes.  Here, we remedy this shortfall by presenting the results from an integrated set of exptl. and computational studies that, for the first time, reveal the storage mechanism for soft carbon.  We find that sodium ions intercalate into graphenic layers, leading to an irreversible quasi-plateau at ∼0.5 V vs. Na+/Na as well as an irreversible expansion seen by in situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and X-ray diffraction (XRD).  Such a high-potential plateau is correlated to the defective local structure inside the turbostratic stacking of soft carbon and the assocd. high-binding energies with Na ions, suggesting a trapping mechanism.  On the other hand, soft carbon exhibits long sloping regions above and below the quasi-plateau during the first sodiation, where the sloping regions present highly reversible behavior.  It is attributed to the more defects contained by soft carbon revealed by neutron total scattering and the assocd. pair distribution function studies.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201800108"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qi, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qi, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Pre-Oxidation-Tuned Microstructures of Carbon Anodes Derived from Pitch for Enhancing Na Storage Performance</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis Strategies and Applications for Pitch-based Anode: From Industrial by-products to Power Sources</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Cen; Yan, Lunjing; Wang, Meijun; Kong, Jiao; Bao, Weiren; Chang, Liping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Record</span>

Moreover, the price of other better-performing carbon materials (such as graphene) is much higher than graphite, which is not conducive to massive prodn.  Pitch, the cheap byproduct in the petroleum and coal industries, has high carbon content and yield, making it possible for commercialization.  Developing pitch-based anodes can not only lower raw material costs but also realize the pitchs high value-added utilization.  We comprehensively reviewed the latest synthesis strategies of pitch-derived materials and then introduced their application and research progress in lithium, sodium, and potassium ion batteries (LIBs, SIBs, and PIBs).                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Boost sodium-ion batteries to commercialization: Strategies to enhance initial Coulombic efficiency of hard carbon anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Minghao; Li, Yu; Wu, Feng; Bai, Ying; Wu, Chuan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are regarded as one of the most promising candidates for large-scale energy storage system due to low cost and inexhaustible sodium reserves.  The commercialize application of SIBs relies on the development of advanced cathode and anode materials.  Among the various available anode materials, hard carbon material is considered to be the most potential anode material, which presents appropriate sodiation/desodiation elec. potential, high capacity, and simple synthesis method.  However, the insufficient initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of hard carbon severely restricts the practical commercialization in SIBs.  Hence, we review the influence elements that cause an inadequate ICE of hard carbon, such as the decompn. of electrolytes, high defects concn., excessive surface functional groups, and irreversible sodium ions and so on.  In order to obtain a moderate ICE, strategies including structure and morphol. modification, defect and surface engineering, compn. adjustment, electrolyte and binder optimization, material pre-treatment are confirmed to be effective.  This review provides a further understanding of obtaining hard carbon electrodes with high ICE, which will contribute to the prosperity of SIBs in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2022.140540"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recyclable molten-salt-assisted synthesis of N-doped porous carbon nanosheets from coal tar pitch for high performance sodium batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Magnesium citrate induced growth of noodle-like porous graphitic carbons from coal tar pitch for high-performance lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xing, Baolin; Zeng, Huihui; Huang, Guangxu; Jia, Jianbo; Yuan, Ruifu; Zhang, Chuanxiang; Sun, Qi; Cao, Yijun; Chen, Zhengfei; Liu, Baozhong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">376</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">138043</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ELCAAV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4686</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Porous graphitic carbons are amongst the most attracted anode materials for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) mainly because of their well-developed porous structure combined with desirable elec. cond.  A novel induced growth strategy is introduced to synthesize noodle-like porous graphitic carbons (NPGCs) from low-cost coal tar pitch (CTP) using Mg citrate as inducer and template.  The as-prepd. NPGCs have unique noodle-like C architecture consisting of cross-linked C chains with large sp. surface area (∼888.1 m2 g-1), high pore vol. (∼2.112 cm3 g-1) as well as desirable cond., and possess hierarchically porous structure contg. favorable distribution of micro-, meso- and macropore and appropriate level of O and N heteroatom doping.  Due to these synergistic microstructure characteristics, the LIB anodes based on NPGCs exhibit remarkably improved electrochem. performance with an av. reversible capacity (1157 mAh g-1 at 0.05 A g-1), superior rate capability (510 mAh g-1 at 2.0 A g-1) and an outstanding long-term cycling performance (95.3% capacity retention after 200 charge-discharge cycles).  This work provides a novel strategy for cost-effective fabrication of more exciting microstructures of NPGCs with adjustable hierarchical porous structure and favorable cond. from cheap C sources for anode materials in high-performance LIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref23" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1063/5.0086359"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Greczynski, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hultman, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A step-by-step guide to perform x-ray photoelectron spectroscopy</span>. <i>J.     <span class="NLM_cas:volume">132</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">011101</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JAPIAU</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0021-8979</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Institute of Physics</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">There is a growing concern within the surface science community that the massive increase in the no. of XPS articles over the last few decades is accompanied by a decrease in work quality including in many cases meaningless chem. bond assignment.  Should this trend continue, it would have disastrous consequences for scientific research.  While there are many factors responsible for this situation, the lack of insight of phys. principles combined with seeming ease of XPS operation and insufficient training are certainly the major ones.  To counter that, we offer a comprehensive tutorial written in the form of a step-by-step guide starting from exptl. planning, through sample selection and handling, instrument setup, data acquisition, spectra anal., and results presentation.  Six application examples highlight the broad range of research questions that can be answered by XPS.  The topic selection and the discussion level are intended to be accessible for novices yet challenging possible preconceptions of experienced practitioners.  The analyses of thin film samples are chosen for model cases as this is from where the bulk of XPS reports presently emanate and also where the author's key expertise lies.  At the same time, the majority of discussed topics is applicable to surface science in general and is, thus, of relevance for the analyses of any type of sample and material class.  The tutorial contains ca. 160 original spectra and over 290 refs. for further reading.  Particular attention is paid to the correct workflow, development of good research practices, and solid knowledge of factors that impact the quality and reliability of the obtained information.  What matters in the end is that the conclusions from the anal. can be trusted.  Our aspiration is that after reading this tutorial each practitioner will be able to perform error-free data anal. and draw meaningful insights from the rich well of XPS.                         cas_cit28R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">28</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Multi-heteroatom doped porous carbon derived from insect feces for capacitance-enhanced sodium-ion storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Chen; Huang, Ying; Meng, Zhuoyue; Xu, Zhipeng; Liu, Panbo; Li, Tiehu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Energy Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">54</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">482-492</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Science Press</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The large-scale application of sodium ion batteries (SIBs) is limited by economic and environmental factors.  Here, we prep. multi-heteroatom self-doped hierarchical porous carbon (HHPC) with a honeycomb-like structure by one-step carbonization method using high-yield and low-cost biomass silkworm excrement as a precursor.  As an anode for SIB, HHPC-1100 exhibits a capacity of 331.7 mA h g-1 at 20 mA g-1, while it also reveals remarkable rate performance and stable long cycle capability due to its abundant pore structure and proper amt. of hetero atom doping.  Moreover, the synergistic effect of O, N, S, P co-doping in carbon materials on sodium ion adsorption is verified by the first-principles study, which provide a theor. basis for the prominent electrochem. performance of the material.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref29" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2022.140573"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Muruganantham, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A green route N, S-doped hard carbon derived from fruit-peel biomass waste as an anode material for rechargeable sodium-ion storage applications</span>. <i>Electrochim.                 10.1016/j.electacta.2022.140573</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D424%26publication_year%3D2022%26journal%3DElectrochim.%2BActa%26author%3DR.%2BMuruganantham%26author%3DF.%2BWang%26author%3DW.%2BLiu%26title%3DA%2Bgreen%2Broute%2BN%252C%2BS-doped%2Bhard%2Bcarbon%2Bderived%2Bfrom%2Bfruit-peel%2Bbiomass%2Bwaste%2Bas%2Ban%2Banode%2Bmaterial%2Bfor%2Brechargeable%2Bsodium-ion%2Bstorage%2Bapplications%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.electacta.2022.140573&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c14597&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.electacta.2022.140573&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A green route N, S-doped hard carbon derived from fruit-peel biomass waste as an anode material for rechargeable sodium-ion storage applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Muruganantham, Rasu; Wang, Fu-Ming; Liu, Wei-Ren</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">424</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">140573</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ELCAAV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4686</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrochem. energy storage devices, namely batteries are vital role to cut greenhouse gas secretions and reliable alternative of fossil fuel-used various elec. energy storage systems.  Thus, the hard carbon is one of the promising cheapest sustainable material for energy storage sectors.  In this study, we assemble the sodium-ion cell via renewable biomass-waste resource of mango-peels derived hard carbon (MPC) used as an anode electrode for Na-ion storage for the first-time.  Furthermore, the hard carbon is modified with N, and S (NS-MPC) through a facile carbonization route.  The structural, functional, morphol. and chem. characteristics are systematically studied in details through spectroscopic techniques.  The NS-MPC electrode consisted sodium-ion storage delivers a reversible discharge capacity of 400 mAh/g at a c.d. of 100 mA/g and exposed a reversible capacity of 155 mAh/g over 2500 cycles at 2 A/g.  The Na-ion charge storage behavior reveals through the different rate controlled cyclic voltammetry (CV) anal., which result exposed increasing contribution percentage of surface-controlled capacity with increment of current rates.  Overall, this work signifies to protecting the environment by employed the bio-wastage to wealth applications, esp. cost-effective fabrication of batteries for future pioneering grid energy storage applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref30" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2019.02.067"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qin, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nitrogen and oxygen co-doping carbon microspheres by a sustainable route for fast sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Electrochim. Acta</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>303</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">140</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">147</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.electacta.2019.02.067</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D303%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D140-147%26journal%3DElectrochim.%2BActa%26author%3DF.%2BZhang%26author%3DD.%2BQin%26author%3DJ.%2BXu%26author%3DZ.%2BLiu%26author%3DY.%2BZhao%26author%3DX.%2BZhang%26title%3DNitrogen%2Band%2Boxygen%2Bco-doping%2Bcarbon%2Bmicrospheres%2Bby%2Ba%2Bsustainable%2Broute%2Bfor%2Bfast%2Bsodium-ion%2Bbatteries%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.electacta.2019.02.067&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c14597&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.electacta.2019.02.067&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nitrogen and oxygen co-doping carbon microspheres by a sustainable route for fast sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Fang; Qin, Decai; Xu, Jiaxin; Liu, Zhanying; Zhao, Yanzhang; Zhang, Xiaogang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">303</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">140-147</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ELCAAV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4686</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Heteroatom-doping was regarded as an effective strategy to regulate the Na storage performance of C materials.  N and O dual-doped C microspheres (NO-CS) from the rinds of corn stalks were fabricated via a tailored carbonization and soln. doping route.  NO-CS reveals both high O (15.6 at.%), N (6.4 at.%) contents and high sp. surface area (746.48 m2 g-1).  The NO-CS derived C anode shows tremendous reversible capacity, rate capability and cycling stability, e.g. 270 mAh g-1 over 200 cycles at 50 mA g-1, 130 mAh g-1 over 3500 cycles at 5 A g-1.  The superior Na storage property may due to the abundant porosity and dual-doping that boost fast ion adsorption at the graphene layer defects and N O moiety of C.  Scalable and cost-effective synthesis route with outstanding Na storage performance makes NO-CS a competitive anode material for Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref31" class="refNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit31" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-018-06144-x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sheng, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lai, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Atomic cobalt as an efficient electrocatalyst in sulfur cathodes for superior room-temperature sodium-sulfur batteries</span>. <i>Nat.                         cas_cit37R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">37</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Boosting sodium storage performance of hard carbons by regulating oxygen functionalities of the cross-linked asphalt precursor</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Ran; Yi, Zonglin; Song, Mingxin; Chen, Jingpeng; Wei, Xianxian; Su, Fangyuan; Dai, Liqin; Sun, Guohua; Yang, Fan; Xie, Lijing; Chen, Cheng-Meng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2023</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">206</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">94-104</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Pre-oxidn. treatment is a key process to produce carbon anodes for sodium ion batteries from pitch because it governs the orientation of carbon layers during subsequent carbonization.  However, the relationship between the oxygen functionalities in the oxidized pitch and the evolution of carbon structure during carbonization remains unclear.  Herein, cross-linked asphalts with differentiated oxygen functionalities such as C-O and C(O)-O (ester group and anhydride), are synthesized to explore their effects on the evolution of carbon structure.  The planar cross-linked structures constructed by thermally stable C-O fail to prevent the sliding of carbon layer, leading the formation of short-range order carbon structures with lager lateral size.  In contrast, the three-dimensional cross-linked structures constructed by C(O)-O effectively prevent the rearrangement of asphalt at early pyrolysis.  Owing to the gas generated by C(O)-O removal during subsequent carbonization, the as-formed more disorder carbon structures with small microcryst. sizes can generate more closed pores and ultra-micropores, which enhances the low-voltage plateau capacity from 96.06 to 132.2 mAh g-1.  The insights for the differentiated oxygen functionalities in regulating the carbon structural conversion pave the way for further development of pitch-based hard carbons with higher performance and their practical applications in SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref38" class="refNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit38" data-doi="10.1007/s12598-024-02793-8"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kuang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Coordination bonds reinforcing mechanical strength of silicon anode to improve the electrochemical stability</span>. <i>Rare Met.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2024</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>43</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">5690</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit39R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">39</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">N, O and S co-doped hierarchical porous carbon derived from a series of samara for lithium and sodium storage: Insights into surface capacitance and inner diffusion</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Jianghua; Zhang, Daile; Li, Kang; Tian, Yaxiong; Wang, Yun; Sun, Ting</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Colloid and Interface Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">598</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Efficiently selecting biomass precursors to prep. porous carbon with rich pore structure and heteroatom doping, and clearly distinguishing the storage behavior of Li+ and Na+ in porous carbon are still the key issues for the development and utilization of biomass-based carbon materials.  In this work, four kinds of samara with a hollow structure are used as carbon sources to prep. an N, O and S co-doped hierarchical porous carbon.  As the anode for Li/Na-ion batteries, the reversible specific capacity of N, O and S co-doped hierarchical porous carbon (HPC-UP-6) is 1072.3 mAh.g-1 (0.0744 A.g-1) and 333.2 mAh.g-1 (0.1 A.g-1), resp.  The ultra-high specific capacity reveals the rationality of preferentially selecting plant fruits with hollow structures as precursors.  In addn., further comparative studies show that the contribution rate of surface-induced capacitance in sodium-ion batteries is more than 10% higher than that in lithium-ion batteries, indicating that Na+ tends to be stored on the surface of porous carbon.  This principle of selecting biomass precursors and the new understanding of the storage mechanism of Li+/Na+ in biomass-based porous carbon can guide the design and prepn. of new carbon materials with high capacity and high-rate performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref40"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref40" class="refNumLink">40</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit40" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2023.142626"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mai, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synergistic S-doped biomass carbon and N-doped carbon for construction of interlayer expanded MoS<sub>2</sub> core/shell nanospheres for potassium and sodium storage</span>. <i>Electrochim.                         cas_cit41R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">41</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tailoring a Phenolic Resin Precursor by Facile Pre-oxidation Tactics to Realize a High-Initial-Coulombic-Efficiency Hard Carbon Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Guifang; Zhang, Lijun; Ren, Qingjuan; Yan, Lei; Zhang, Fuming; Lv, Wenjie; Shi, Zhiqiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">As the leading anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), hard carbon (HC) still faces the puzzle of low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) in achieving commercialization.  From the perspective of precursors, the low ICE has been attributed to the large sp. surface area and porosity produced by the rapid decompn. of polymers during the carbonization.  Therefore, increasing the crosslinking degree of precursors will be an effective shortcut to improve the ICE.  Herein, a facile pre-oxidn. tactic was successfully employed to tailor the crosslinking degree of phenolic resin precursors to precisely control the sp. surface area of the obtained HC.  As the pre-oxidn. time is increased, the optimal HC with the lowest sp. surface area shows an ICE elevated by 22.2% (from 62.5 to 84.7%) compared to the original pre-oxidn. HC and delivers a high reversible capacity of 334.3 mAh g-1 at 20 mA g-1.  Besides, the pre-oxidn. also introduces abundant carbonyl groups, which increase the disorder degree of HC and supply abundant adsorption sites of Na+, thus enhancing the rate performance.  When matched with a layered O3-NaNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2 cathode, the full cell achieves an energy d. of ca. 256.2 Wh kg-1 with superior rate performance.  This work sheds light on the pos. effect of pre-oxidn. in elevating the ICE of HC and provides effective guidance to achieve a high ICE for other HC materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref42" class="refNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit42" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202000283"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Alvin, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cahyadi, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kwak, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Revealing the Intercalation Mechanisms of Lithium, Sodium, and Potassium in Hard Carbon</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Revealing the Intercalation Mechanisms of Lithium, Sodium, and Potassium in Hard Carbon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Alvin, Stevanus; Cahyadi, Handi Setiadi; Hwang, Jieun; Chang, Wonyoung; Kwak, Sang Kyu; Kim, Jaehoon</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">20</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2000283</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon is the most promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries and potassium-ion batteries owing to its high stability, widespread availability, low-cost, and excellent performance.  Understanding the carrier-ion storage mechanism is a prerequisite for developing high-performance electrode materials; however, the underlying ion storage mechanism in hard carbon has been a topic of debate because of its complex structure.  Herein, it is demonstrated that the Li+-, Na+-, and K+-ion storage mechanisms in hard carbon are based on the adsorption of ions on the surface of active sites (e.g., deffects, edges, and residual heteroatoms) in the sloping voltage region, followed by intercalation into the graphitic layers in the low-voltage plateau region.  At a low c.d. of 3 mA g-1, the graphitic layers of hard carbon are unlocked to permit Li+-ion intercalation, resulting in a plateau region in the lithium-ion batteries.  To gain insights into the ion storage mechanism, exptl. observations including various ex situ techniques, a const.-current const.-voltage method, and diffusivity measurements are correlated with the theor. estn. of changes in carbon structures and insertion voltages during ion insertion obtained using the d. functional theory.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref43" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1021/acsaenm.3c00349"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Slope-Dominated N/S Co-Doped Carbon Anode Derived from Pitch for Sodium-ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0935-9648</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon attracts considerable attention as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries; however, their poor rate capability and low realistic capacity have motivated intense research effort toward exploiting nanostructured carbons in order to boost their comprehensive performance.  Ultramicropores are considered essential for attaining high-rate capacity as well as initial Coulombic efficiency by allowing the rapid diffusion of Na+ and inhibiting the contact of the electrolyte with the inner carbon surfaces.  Herein, hard carbon nanosheets with centralized ultramicropores (≈ 0.5 nm) and easily accessible carbonyl groups (C=O)/hydroxy groups (O-H) are synthesized via interfacial assembly and carbonization strategies, delivering a large capacity (318 mA h g-1 at 0.02 A g-1), superior rate capability (145 mA h g-1 at 5.00 A g-1), and approx. 95% of reversible capacity &lt; 1.00 V.  Notably, a new charge model favoring fast capacitive sodium storage with dual potential plateaus is proposed.  That is, the deintercalation of Na+ from graphitic layers is manifested as the low-potential plateau region (0.01-0.10 V), contributing to stable insertion capacity; meanwhile, the surface desodiation process of the C=O and O-H groups corresponds to the high-potential plateau region (0.40-0.70 V), contributing to a fast capacitive storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref45" class="refNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit45" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2024.119165"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yi, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tian, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Su, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Insight into the effect of structural differences among pitch fractions on sodium storage performance of pitch-derived hard carbons</span>. <i>Carbon</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2024</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>226</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">119165</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.carbon.2024.119165</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D226%26publication_year%3D2024%26journal%3DCarbon%26author%3DX.%2BZhang%26author%3DZ.%2BYi%26author%3DY.%2BTian%26author%3DL.%2BXie%26author%3DF.%2BSu%26author%3DX.%2BWei%26author%3DJ.%2BChen%26author%3DC.%2BChen%26title%3DInsight%2Binto%2Bthe%2Beffect%2Bof%2Bstructural%2Bdifferences%2Bamong%2Bpitch%2Bfractions%2Bon%2Bsodium%2Bstorage%2Bperformance%2Bof%2Bpitch-derived%2Bhard%2Bcarbons%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.carbon.2024.119165&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c14597&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.carbon.2024.119165&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord empty" id="

                10.1039/D3CS00601H</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c01604"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis of KVPO<sub>4</sub>F/Carbon Porous Single Crystalline Nanoplates for High-Rate Potassium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nano Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>22</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">12</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">4933</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">4940</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.nanolett.2c01604</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis of KVPO4F/Carbon Porous Single Crystalline Nanoplates for High-Rate Potassium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liao, Jiaying; Zhang, Xinxin; Zhang, Qinghua; Hu, Qiao; Li, Yafei; Du, Yichen; Xu, Jianzhi; Gu, Lin; Zhou, Xiaosi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">22</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4933-4940</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">With high theor. capacity and operating voltage, KVPO4F is a potential high energy d. cathode material for potassium-ion batteries.  However, its performance is usually limited by F loss, poor electronic cond., and unsteady electrode/electrolyte interface.  Herein, a simple one-step sintering process is developed, where vanadium-oxalate-phosphite/phosphate frameworks and fluorinated polymer are used to synthesize carbon-coated KVPO4F nanoplates.  It is found that the V-F-C bond generated by fluorinated-polymer-derived carbon at the interface of KVPO4F/C nanoplates diminishes the F loss, as well as enhances K-ions migration ability and the electronic cond. of KVPO4F.  The as-synthesized KVPO4F/C cathode delivers a reversible capacity of 106.5 mAh g-1 at 0.2 C, a high working voltage of 4.28 V, and a rate capability with capacity of 73.8 mAh g-1 at the ultrahigh c.d. of 100 C. Owing to the shortcomings of traditional electrode materials in alkali metal-ion batteries (AIBs), such as limited reversible specific capacity, low power d., and poor cycling performance, it is particularly important to develop new electrode materials.  Covalent org. frameworks (COFs) are cryst. porous polymers that incorporate org. building blocks into their periodic structures through dynamic covalent bonds.  COFs are superior to org. materials because of their high designability, regular channels, and stable topol.  Since the first report of DTP-ANDI-COF as a cathode material for lithium-ion batteries in 2015, research on COF electrode materials has made continuous progress and breakthroughs. In the present work, natural graphite (G) is reported not only to suffer from low capacity during fast charging, but also from charge failure after many charging cycles.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite is the com. anode for lithium-ion batteries; however, it fails to extend its success to sodium-ion batteries.  Recently, we demonstrated that a low-cost amorphous carbon-soft carbon exhibits remarkable rate performance and stable cycling life of Na-ion storage.  However, its Na-ion storage mechanism has remained elusive, which has plagued further development of such carbon anodes.  Here, we remedy this shortfall by presenting the results from an integrated set of exptl. and computational studies that, for the first time, reveal the storage mechanism for soft carbon.  We find that sodium ions intercalate into graphenic layers, leading to an irreversible quasi-plateau at ∼0.5 V vs. Na+/Na as well as an irreversible expansion seen by in situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and X-ray diffraction (XRD).  Such a high-potential plateau is correlated to the defective local structure inside the turbostratic stacking of soft carbon and the assocd. high-binding energies with Na ions, suggesting a trapping mechanism.  On the other hand, soft carbon exhibits long sloping regions above and below the quasi-plateau during the first sodiation, where the sloping regions present highly reversible behavior. Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are regarded as one of the most promising candidates for large-scale energy storage system due to low cost and inexhaustible sodium reserves.  The commercialize application of SIBs relies on the development of advanced cathode and anode materials.  Among the various available anode materials, hard carbon material is considered to be the most potential anode material, which presents appropriate sodiation/desodiation elec. potential, high capacity, and simple synthesis method.  However, the insufficient initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of hard carbon severely restricts the practical commercialization in SIBs.  Hence, we review the influence elements that cause an inadequate ICE of hard carbon, such as the decompn. of electrolytes, high defects concn., excessive surface functional groups, and irreversible sodium ions and so on.  In order to obtain a moderate ICE, strategies including structure and morphol. modification, defect and surface engineering, compn. adjustment, electrolyte and binder optimization, material pre-treatment are confirmed to be effective.                         rightTab-cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Magnesium citrate induced growth of noodle-like porous graphitic carbons from coal tar pitch for high-performance lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xing, Baolin; Zeng, Huihui; Huang, Guangxu; Jia, Jianbo; Yuan, Ruifu; Zhang, Chuanxiang; Sun, Qi; Cao, Yijun; Chen, Zhengfei; Liu, Baozhong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Porous graphitic carbons are amongst the most attracted anode materials for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) mainly because of their well-developed porous structure combined with desirable elec. cond.  A novel induced growth strategy is introduced to synthesize noodle-like porous graphitic carbons (NPGCs) from low-cost coal tar pitch (CTP) using Mg citrate as inducer and template.  The as-prepd. NPGCs have unique noodle-like C architecture consisting of cross-linked C chains with large sp. surface area (∼888.1 m2 g-1), high pore vol. (∼2.112 cm3 g-1) as well as desirable cond., and possess hierarchically porous structure contg. favorable distribution of micro-, meso- and macropore and appropriate level of O and N heteroatom doping.  Due to these synergistic microstructure characteristics, the LIB anodes based on NPGCs exhibit remarkably improved electrochem. performance with an av. reversible capacity (1157 mAh g-1 at 0.05 A g-1), superior rate capability (510 mAh g-1 at 2.0 A g-1) and an outstanding long-term cycling performance (95.3% capacity retention after 200 charge-discharge cycles).  This work provides a novel strategy for cost-effective fabrication of more exciting microstructures of NPGCs with adjustable hierarchical porous structure and favorable cond. from cheap C sources for anode materials in high-performance LIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref23" class="rightTabRefNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit23" data-doi="10.1063/5.0086359"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Greczynski, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hultman, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A step-by-step guide to perform x-ray photoelectron spectroscopy</span>. <i>J. Appl. Phys.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>132</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">011101</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1063/5.0086359</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A step-by-step guide to perform x-ray photoelectron spectroscopy</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Greczynski, Grzegorz; Hultman, Lars</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Applied Physics (Melville, NY, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">132</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Institute of Physics</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">There is a growing concern within the surface science community that the massive increase in the no. of XPS articles over the last few decades is accompanied by a decrease in work quality including in many cases meaningless chem. bond assignment.  Should this trend continue, it would have disastrous consequences for scientific research.  While there are many factors responsible for this situation, the lack of insight of phys. principles combined with seeming ease of XPS operation and insufficient training are certainly the major ones.  To counter that, we offer a comprehensive tutorial written in the form of a step-by-step guide starting from exptl. planning, through sample selection and handling, instrument setup, data acquisition, spectra anal., and results presentation.  Six application examples highlight the broad range of research questions that can be answered by XPS.  The topic selection and the discussion level are intended to be accessible for novices yet challenging possible preconceptions of experienced practitioners.  The analyses of thin film samples are chosen for model cases as this is from where the bulk of XPS reports presently emanate and also where the author's key expertise lies.  At the same time, the majority of discussed topics is applicable to surface science in general and is, thus, of relevance for the analyses of any type of sample and material class.  The tutorial contains ca. 160 original spectra and over 290 refs. for further reading.  Particular attention is paid to the correct workflow, development of good research practices, and solid knowledge of factors that impact the quality and reliability of the obtained information.  What matters in the end is that the conclusions from the anal. can be trusted.  Our aspiration is that after reading this tutorial each practitioner will be able to perform error-free data anal. and draw meaningful insights from the rich well of XPS.     </div><div class="casAbstract">The low-cost room-temperature sodium-sulfur battery system is arousing extensive interest owing to its promise for large-scale applications.  Although significant efforts have been made, resolving low sulfur reaction activity and severe polysulfide dissolution remains challenging.  Here, a sulfur host comprised of atomic cobalt-decorated hollow carbon nanospheres is synthesized to enhance sulfur reactivity and to electrocatalytically reduce polysulfide into the final product, sodium sulfide.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Pre-oxidn. treatment is a key process to produce carbon anodes for sodium ion batteries from pitch because it governs the orientation of carbon layers during subsequent carbonization.  However, the relationship between the oxygen functionalities in the oxidized pitch and the evolution of carbon structure during carbonization remains unclear.  Herein, cross-linked asphalts with differentiated oxygen functionalities such as C-O and C(O)-O (ester group and anhydride), are synthesized to explore their effects on the evolution of carbon structure.         </div><div class="casAbstract">As the leading anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), hard carbon (HC) still faces the puzzle of low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) in achieving commercialization.  From the perspective of precursors, the low ICE has been attributed to the large sp. surface area and porosity produced by the rapid decompn. of polymers during the carbonization.  Therefore, increasing the crosslinking degree of precursors will be an effective shortcut to improve the ICE.  Herein, a facile pre-oxidn. tactic was successfully employed to tailor the crosslinking degree of phenolic resin precursors to precisely control the sp. surface area of the obtained HC.  As the pre-oxidn. time is increased, the optimal HC with the lowest sp. surface area shows an ICE elevated by 22.2% (from 62.5 to 84.7%) compared to the original pre-oxidn. HC and delivers a high reversible capacity of 334.3 mAh g-1 at 20 mA g-1.  Besides, the pre-oxidn. also introduces abundant carbonyl groups, which increase the disorder degree of HC and supply abundant adsorption sites of Na+, thus enhancing the rate performance.  When matched with a layered O3-NaNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2 cathode, the full cell achieves an energy d. of ca. 256.2 Wh kg-1 with superior rate performance.  This work sheds light on the pos. effect of pre-oxidn. in elevating the ICE of HC and provides effective guidance to achieve a high ICE for other HC materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref42" class="rightTabRefNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit42" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202000283"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Alvin, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cahyadi, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kwak, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Revealing the Intercalation Mechanisms of Lithium, Sodium, and Potassium in Hard Carbon</span>. <i>Adv. Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>10</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">2000283</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.202000283</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Revealing the Intercalation Mechanisms of Lithium, Sodium, and Potassium in Hard Carbon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Alvin, Stevanus; Cahyadi, Handi Setiadi; Hwang, Jieun; Chang, Wonyoung; Kwak, Sang Kyu; Kim, Jaehoon</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">20</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2000283</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon is the most promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries and potassium-ion batteries owing to its high stability, widespread availability, low-cost, and excellent performance.  Understanding the carrier-ion storage mechanism is a prerequisite for developing high-performance electrode materials; however, the underlying ion storage mechanism in hard carbon has been a topic of debate because of its complex structure.  Herein, it is demonstrated that the Li+-, Na+-, and K+-ion storage mechanisms in hard carbon are based on the adsorption of ions on the surface of active sites (e.g., deffects, edges, and residual heteroatoms) in the sloping voltage region, followed by intercalation into the graphitic layers in the low-voltage plateau region.  At a low c.d. of 3 mA g-1, the graphitic layers of hard carbon are unlocked to permit Li+-ion intercalation, resulting in a plateau region in the lithium-ion batteries.  To gain insights into the ion storage mechanism, exptl. observations including various ex situ techniques, a const.-current const.-voltage method, and diffusivity measurements are correlated with the theor. estn. of changes in carbon structures and insertion voltages during ion insertion obtained using the d. functional theory.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref43" class="rightTabRefNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit43" data-doi="10.1021/acsaenm.3c00349"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Slope-Dominated N/S Co-Doped Carbon Anode Derived from Pitch for Sodium-ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl. Eng. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2023</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>1</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">10</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">2524</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2532</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acsaenm.3c00349</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref44" class="rightTabRefNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit44" data-doi="10.1002/adma.202000447"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dong, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hard carbon nanosheets with uniform ultramicropores and accessible functional groups showing high realistic capacity and superior rate performance for sodium-ion storage</span>. <i>Adv. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>32</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">2000447</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adma.202000447</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hard Carbon Nanosheets with Uniform Ultramicropores and Accessible Functional Groups Showing High Realistic Capacity and Superior Rate Performance for Sodium-Ion Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xia, Ji-Li; Yan, Dong; Guo, Li-Ping; Dong, Xiao-Ling; Li, Wen-Cui; Lu, An-Hui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">32</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">21</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2000447</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADVMEW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0935-9648</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon attracts considerable attention as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries; however, their poor rate capability and low realistic capacity have motivated intense research effort toward exploiting nanostructured carbons in order to boost their comprehensive performance.  Ultramicropores are considered essential for attaining high-rate capacity as well as initial Coulombic efficiency by allowing the rapid diffusion of Na+ and inhibiting the contact of the electrolyte with the inner carbon surfaces.  Herein, hard carbon nanosheets with centralized ultramicropores (≈ 0.5 nm) and easily accessible carbonyl groups (C=O)/hydroxy groups (O-H) are synthesized via interfacial assembly and carbonization strategies, delivering a large capacity (318 mA h g-1 at 0.02 A g-1), superior rate capability (145 mA h g-1 at 5.00 A g-1), and approx. 95% of reversible capacity &lt; 1.00 V.  Notably, a new charge model favoring fast capacitive sodium storage with dual potential plateaus is proposed.  That is, the deintercalation of Na+ from graphitic layers is manifested as the low-potential plateau region (0.01-0.10 V), contributing to stable insertion capacity; meanwhile, the surface desodiation process of the C=O and O-H groups corresponds to the high-potential plateau region (0.40-0.70 V), contributing to a fast capacitive storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref45" class="rightTabRefNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit45" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2024.119165"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yi, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tian, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Su, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Insight into the effect of structural differences among pitch fractions on sodium storage performance of pitch-derived hard carbons</span>. <i>Carbon</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2024</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>226</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">119165</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.carbon.2024.119165</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i13">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c14597?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c14597</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Lattice parameters and pores, element content of NS-P-OPDC and its transformation into an anode material after cycling, morphology analyses of templates, CV curves in first three cycles at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, and Nyquist plots by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c14597/suppl_file/am4c14597_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c14597/suppl_file/am4c14597_si_001.pdf">am4c14597_si_001.pdf (417.3 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	});

		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

<script nonce="93188594997754b8-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>

<script nonce="93188594997754b8-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage =
          "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

      function init() {
        setTimeout(function () {
          var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
            return false;
          } else {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
            gdprMessage();
          }
          //Events
          $("#closebtn").on("click", hideForm);

          //Handle submit
          $("#gdpr-message-btn").on("click", function (event) {
            handleClick();
          });

          // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
          // $(document).click(function(event) {
          // 	event.stopPropagation();
          //   console.log(event.target);
          //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
          //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
          //     $(this).off();
          //   }
          // });
          $("#gdpr-con-btn").click(function () {
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            hideForm();
          });
        }, time);
      }

      //Show the message
      function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $("#gdpr-message-modal").show();
      }

      function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
          //Submit the form
          //Hide form
          if ($(".gdpr_err")) {
            $(".gdpr_err").hide();
          }

          $("#gdpr-message-content");
          //If successful then...
          setCookie("gdpr", "true");
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
          //Show error message
          gdprErrorMessage();
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
      }

      function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
          return false;
        } else {
          errorStatus = true;
          $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
      }

      function validateEmail(email) {
        var re =
          /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

  </script>
</p>

var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>
